Now, we also need to consider the possibility of cycles of length >1. The problem asks for all possible a1 such that the infinite sequence exists. So we need to find all a1 for which the recursion never hits a number with fewer than three proper divisors. So we need to characterize the set of initial numbers that lead to an infinite sequence under iteration of f. This is like a dynamical system on positive integers with at least three proper divisors. We need to find all numbers that never map to a number with fewer than three proper divisors. In other words, the forward orbit stays in the set of numbers with at least three proper divisors.

We have found some fixed points: numbers N=2*3^k (k>=1). Also, maybe there are numbers that eventually map to such fixed points, and then stay constant. Those would also be valid a1 because the sequence is infinite (it becomes constant after some steps). So we need to find all a1 such that the iteration never leads to a "dead end" (a prime or a square of a prime). Possibly the only numbers that never lead to a dead end are those that are powers of 2? Or something like that. Let's explore.

We need to determine all a1 such that the forward orbit stays in S (numbers with at least three proper divisors). Since we have many fixed points, the dynamics is like: eventually either hit a fixed point (which then stays constant) or hit a prime or a prime square (which then terminates). Since f is defined only for numbers with at least three proper divisors, we can consider the functional graph.

Observation: For any number N with at least three proper divisors, we can compute f(N). The fixed points are exactly numbers N such that v2(N)=1 and 5∤N (and also divisible by 3? Actually N must be divisible by 6 because if N is a fixed point, we derived that 1/p+1/d3+1/d4=1 with p=2, d3=3, d4=6. That implies N is divisible by 2 and 3, and also that there is no divisor between 3 and 6. So N must be a multiple of 6, and not divisible by 4 or 5. So N = 6 * k, where k is odd and not divisible by 5. So k can be any odd integer coprime to 5. So the fixed points are exactly numbers N with v2(N)=1 and v5(N)=0 and N≡0 mod 3? Actually N divisible by 3 is automatic because N=6k, so divisible by 3. So yes, fixed points: N=2*3^k * m, where m is coprime to 6 and not divisible by 5. But we can also have N=2*3^1 * m where m is any integer coprime to 6 and not divisible by 5. However, note that if m contains a factor 3? That would increase the exponent of 3. But m is coprime to 6, so cannot contain 2 or 3. So m is product of primes >=7. So N=2*3^a * m, with a>=1, m coprime to 6, 5∤m. But also, if a=1 and m=1, N=6; if a=2 and m=1, N=18; if a=3 and m=1, N=54; etc. So fixed points include all numbers of the form 2*3^a (a>=1) and also 2*3^a * p where p is prime >=7, p≠5. And also products of multiple such primes.

For instance, consider a fixed point F=6*13=78. We need N such that f(N)=78. Let's try to find N. We can set up: sum of three largest proper divisors of N = 78. We can try to find N. Since f(N) is at most N/2+N/3+N/4? Actually the three largest proper divisors are at most N/2, N/3, N/4? Now, we can try to find integer x such that x divides N, and N = p x, with p prime, and x is the largest proper divisor. Also, we need that the other proper divisors y and z are such that y = p x / q, z = p x / r, where q and r are the next smallest divisors. This is complicated.

Maybe we can characterize all N for which the sequence is infinite by looking at the prime factorization. The recursion involves the three smallest divisors. Perhaps the only way to avoid hitting a prime or a prime square is to have a factorization that ensures that the three largest proper divisors sum to a number that is again composite and with at least three proper divisors. This seems like a self-similar property.

Let's analyze the condition for N to have at least three proper divisors. That means N is not prime and not a square of a prime. So N must be composite and not of the form p^2. So N can be p^3, p*q, p^2*q, etc. For N = p^3, f(N)=p^2+p+1. For N = p*q, f(N)=p+q+1. For N = p^2*q, f(N)=? Let's compute: N = p^2*q, with p<q primes? Could be any primes. The divisors: 1, p, p^2, q, p q, p^2 q. The three smallest divisors >1: p, p^2, q? Actually if q < p^2, then the order is 1, p, q, p^2? But p^2 might be greater than q if q is small. So we need to consider cases. For N = 2^2*3=12, we saw f=13. For N = 2^2*5=20, f=19. For N = 3^2*2=18, f=18. So it varies.

Maybe we can derive a condition on the sum of three largest proper divisors that ensures that the sequence continues. Possibly the only numbers that work are those that are multiples of 6 and also have v2(N)=1 or v2(N)=3 and v3(N)>=2? But then we need to check if numbers with v2=3 and v3>=2 map to fixed points. Already verified 72,216,504, etc. Are there numbers with v2=3 and v3=2 that are not of the form 8*9*m? Actually 8*9=72, and m coprime to 30. So they are of that form. So the set of numbers that map to fixed points in one step is: N = 2^3 * 3^b * m, with b>=2, gcd(m,30)=1. But note: these numbers themselves are not necessarily fixed points; they map to numbers of the form 2*13*3^{b-1}*m, which are fixed points. So these N are good. Now, could there be numbers that map to these N? That would be numbers whose f is of that form. Those would also be good. So we need to find all numbers that eventually reach a fixed point. This could be an infinite set with a simple characterization: maybe all numbers that are divisible by 3 and not equal to 1? But we saw 15 fails. However, 15 is divisible by 3 but not by 2. So maybe the condition is that N must be even? But 24 is even and fails. So not.

Let's compute f for some numbers that are multiples of 3 but not multiples of 2: 15->9 (dead), 21->11 (dead), 27->13 (dead), 33->15->9 dead, 39->17 dead, 45->29 dead, 51->21->11 dead, 57->23 dead, 63->37 dead, 69->27->13 dead, 75->45->29 dead, 81->39->17 dead, 87->33->15->9 dead, 93->35->13 dead, 99->53 dead, 105->71? Actually 105's f=71, which is prime, dead. So all these lead to prime or prime square. So they are all dead ends. So no odd multiple of 3 works.

Now consider numbers that are multiples of 6 but not of the form 2*3^b*m with b>=1, m coprime to 6? Actually all multiples of 6 have v2>=1, v3>=1. Now, we need to present the solution in a rigorous proof. The problem asks: "Determine all possible values of a1." So the answer should be: All positive integers N such that N is divisible by 6, N is not divisible by 5, and either the exponent of 2 in N is 1, or the exponent of 2 is 3 and the exponent of 3 is at least 2. But we can also describe the set as: N = 2 * 3^k * m, where k >= 1, m is a positive integer coprime to 6, and 5 ∤ N; or N = 2^3 * 3^k * m, where k >= 2, m is coprime to 6, and 5 ∤ N. Actually note that for the first family, k can be any positive integer; for the second, k >= 2. Also, m is any integer coprime to 6 (i.e., gcd(m,6)=1) and also not divisible by 5. So m is coprime to 30.

But we might also need to consider numbers that are not of the form 2*3^k*m? Actually all multiples of 6 can be written as 2*3*k. But our condition picks out those with v2=1 or v2=3 and v3>=2. So the answer is a union of two infinite families.

Now, we should also verify that all numbers in these families indeed lead to infinite sequences. We already argued that for numbers in (A) (v2=1, 3|N, 5∤N), f(N)=N, so the sequence is constant, hence infinite. For numbers in (B) (v2=3, v3>=2, 5∤N), we have f(N)=13N/12, which is of the form 2*13*3^{b-1}*m, and this number has v2=1, v3>=1, and no factor 5, so it is a fixed point. Thus the sequence becomes constant after one step, hence infinite. So these are all valid.

Now, we also need to show that any other a1 leads to a dead end (i.e., eventually a number with fewer than three proper divisors). That is, if a1 does not satisfy the conditions, then the sequence eventually hits a prime or a prime square. This requires a proof. We can attempt to prove that if N has at least three proper divisors and does not satisfy the conditions, then either f(N) is a prime or a prime square, or the process eventually reaches such. But we need to be careful: there could be cycles of length >1 that avoid primes? We have not found any cycles. Could there be a cycle like N->M->N with both having at least three proper divisors? Thus it seems that the only numbers that do not lead to dead ends are those that map to fixed points after at most one step. And these are precisely the numbers we characterized. But we need to prove that if a1 does not satisfy those conditions, then the sequence eventually reaches a number with fewer than three proper divisors. This is a nontrivial part. The typical solution might involve analyzing the behavior of the sequence in terms of the prime factorization and showing that if the number is not of the form described, then either f(N) is a prime or a prime square, or the process reduces the "size" of N in some measure that eventually forces a prime. We need to find a rigorous argument.

Let's try to define a measure that decreases until we hit a prime or a prime square. For numbers with at least three proper divisors, consider the sum of the three largest proper divisors. For numbers that are not fixed points, we might be able to show that f(N) < N or f(N) > N, but not sure.

Observation: If N is even and not divisible by 3, then N/2 is a proper divisor, and the next largest proper divisor is at most N/4 (since if N is divisible by 4, then N/4 is a divisor; if not, the next divisor is N/3? But if N not divisible by 3, the second smallest divisor could be 5? Actually if N is even and not divisible by 3, then the smallest prime factor is 2, and the next smallest divisor could be 4 (if N divisible by 4) or 5 (if N divisible by 5) or something. But then f(N) = N/2 + N/q + N/r. Since N/q and N/r are at most N/4 and N/5? Actually they could be larger if q is small. But note that if N is even and not divisible by 3, then the three smallest divisors are: 2, then possibly 4 (if 4|N) or 5 (if 5|N) or 7, etc. So the sum could be as large as N/2+N/4+N/5 = 0.5N+0.25N+0.2N=1.0N? Actually 0.5+0.25+0.2=0.95N, which is less than N. So f(N) < N. So for even numbers not divisible by 3, f(N) < N. For even numbers divisible by 3, we have p=2, q=3, r=4 if N divisible by 4? But if N is divisible by 4 and 3, then v2>=2, v3>=1. Then the three smallest divisors: 2,3,4. Then f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/4 = (6N+4N+3N)/12 = 13N/12 > N. So for numbers with v2>=2 and v3>=1, f(N) > N. For numbers with v2=1 and v3>=1, then the three smallest divisors: 2,3,6? Actually if v2=1, then N=2*odd, and if divisible by 3, then the divisors are 1,2,3,6,... So p=2, q=3, r=6. Then f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/6 = N, so fixed. For numbers with v2=0 (odd), then p>=3. For odd N divisible by 3, then p=3, q=3? Actually the smallest divisor >1 is 3. Then the next smallest divisor could be 3? No, divisors are distinct. So if N is divisible by 3, the second smallest divisor is the next smallest divisor after 3, which could be 5 if 5|N, or 9 if 9|N and no 5, etc. So we need to analyze the sum. For odd N divisible by 3, we have p=3, q could be 5,9, etc. Then f(N) = N/3 + N/q + N/r. Since N/3 is at most N/3, and N/q and N/r are at most N/5 and N/7? Actually if q=5, then N/5 is smaller. So f(N) <= N/3+N/5+N/7 = (35N+21N+15N)/105 = 71N/105 ≈ 0.676N < N. So for odd N divisible by 3, f(N) < N. For odd N not divisible by 3, the smallest prime factor p>=5, then f(N) <= N/5+N/7+N/11 < N, so f(N) < N. So in general, f(N) < N unless N is even and divisible by 3 with v2>=2? Actually we saw that for even N divisible by 3, f(N) can be >N if v2>=2, or =N if v2=1, or <N if v2=0? But v2=0 means odd, we already covered. For even N not divisible by 3, f(N)<N. For even N divisible by 3, if v2=1, f=N; if v2>=2, f>N. So the only cases where f(N) >= N are when N is even and divisible by 3 with v2>=1? Actually v2=1 gives equality; v2>=2 gives >N. So for even numbers divisible by 3, f(N) >= N. For other numbers, f(N) < N.

Thus, if a1 is not a fixed point and not in (B), then either f(N) < N, or f(N) > N? Let's see: For numbers in (A) (v2=1, v3>=1, 5∤N), f(N)=N, so they are fixed. 2. Characterize the three largest proper divisors in terms of the smallest divisors. If the divisors of N are sorted ascending: 1=d1<d2<...<dk=N, then the three largest proper divisors are N/d2, N/d3, N/d4. So f(N)=N/d2+N/d3+N/d4, where d2,d3,d4 are the three smallest divisors >1.

3. Let p=d2 be the smallest prime factor. Then p is prime. Write N=p^α·q^β·... where q≥p. Then d3 is the next smallest divisor after p. It could be either p^2 (if p^2 divides N and p^2<q) or q (if q<p^2). Similarly, d4 is the next after d3.

4. Show that if N is divisible by 3 and has v2(N)=1, then the three smallest divisors are 2,3,6. So f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/6=N. Thus N is a fixed point, so the sequence is constant, hence infinite.

Now, we can present the solution.

Let's write the proof:

- Step 1: For any N with at least three proper divisors, define the three smallest divisors >1 as p, q, r (with p≤q≤r). Then f(N)=N/p+N/q+N/r.

- Step 2: Show that if p=2 and q=3 and r=6, then f(N)=N. This happens when N is even, divisible by 3, and not divisible by 4 or 5? Actually we need to ensure that the next divisor after 2 is 3 (so N not divisible by 4? Actually if N is divisible by 4, then 4 is a divisor and is less than 3? No, 4>3. So the order is 2,3,4 if 4 divides N and N is divisible by 3. So for p=2, q=3, r=6, we need that 4 does not divide N (i.e., v2(N)=1) and also that 5 does not divide N (so that 6 is the next after 3? Actually if 5 divides N, then 5 is a divisor, and since 5<6, the order would be 2,3,5,... So r would be 5, not 6. So we need v2(N)=1 and 5∤N. So the fixed points are exactly numbers N with 6|N, v2(N)=1, 5∤N. (Note: v2=1 automatically implies N is not divisible by 4, so no 4 factor; also 5∤N.)

- Step 3: Show that if p=2 and q=3 and r=4, then f(N)=13N/12. This happens when N is even, divisible by 3, and divisible by 4 (v2≥2). So v2(N)≥2 and v3(N)≥1. Then N=2^a·3^b·m with a≥2, b≥1, gcd(m,6)=1. Then f(N)=13·2^{a-2}·3^{b-1}·m.

- Step 4: Consider the sequence a_n. If a_1 is in the fixed point set (v2=1, 3|N, 5∤N), then a_n constant, infinite. If a_1 is in the set P = { N : v2(N)=3, v3(N)≥2, 5∤N }, then a_2 = 13·2·3^{b-1}·m = 2·13·3^{b-1}·m, which has v2=1, v3≥1, 5∤, so a_2 is a fixed point, thus a_n constant from n=2 onward. So these a_1 work.

- Step 5: Now suppose a_1 is not in these sets. Then consider the sequence. We claim that it eventually hits a number with fewer than three proper divisors.

   Case A: a_1 is not divisible by 3. Then the smallest prime factor p≥2 (if a_1 even) or p≥5 (if a_1 odd). In both subcases, f(a_1) < a_1. (Prove: if a_1 even, p=2; then the next divisor q is at least 4 if 4|a_1, or at least 5 if not; in either case, f(a_1) ≤ a_1/2 + a_1/4 + a_1/5 = 0.95a_1 < a_1. If a_1 odd, p≥5, then f(a_1) ≤ a_1/5 + a_1/7 + a_1/11 < a_1.) Thus the sequence strictly decreases until it reaches a number with fewer than three proper divisors. Since the sequence consists of positive integers, it must eventually become a prime or a prime square, which have fewer than three proper divisors. So the sequence cannot be infinite.

   Case B: a_1 is divisible by 3. Then consider v2(a_1)=a. If a=1, then a_1 is in the fixed point set, already covered. If a≥2, then a_2 = f(a_1) = 13·2^{a-2}·3^{b-1}·m, where b=v3(a_1)≥1. So a_2 > a_1. Now, we iterate. Define a sequence of numbers. If a_2 has v2=1 and v3≥1, then a_2 is a fixed point, so the process ends. If a_2 has v2≥2 and v3≥1, then we can repeat: a_3 = 13·2^{a-4}·3^{b-2}·m. Continue this until either v2 becomes 1 or v3 becomes 1. Because each step reduces the exponent of 2 by 2 and exponent of 3 by 1, after a finite number of steps we will reach a number with either v2=1 or v3=1 (or both). If we reach v2=1, then the number is a fixed point (since v3≥1? Actually if v2=1, then the number is of the form 2·3^k·m with k≥1, and we need to also ensure 5∤N. If 5 divides this number, then it's not in the fixed point set. But wait, if v2=1 and v3≥1, but also 5 divides N, then the number is not a fixed point because f(N)=31N/30 > N? Actually we need to check: If v2=1 and v3≥1, but also 5|N, then the smallest divisors are 2,3,5, so f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/5 = 31N/30 > N. So it's not a fixed point; it would increase. But then we would have a number with v2=1, v3≥1, 5|N, which is not of the form we considered earlier. So we need to analyze that possibility.

   Let's re-examine: Our earlier classification of fixed points assumed that the three smallest divisors are 2,3,6. This requires v2=1 and 5∤N. If 5 divides N, then the third smallest divisor is 5 (since 3 is a divisor, 5 is also a divisor, and 4 does not divide because v2=1). So the order is 2,3,5. Then f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/5 = 31N/30. This is >N. So then N increases. But then we get a number that is of the form 31·2^{a-2}·3^{b-1}·m? Actually N=2·3^b·5·c? Let's be systematic.

   We need to cover all numbers that are not in the good families. In our case analysis, we considered numbers with v2=1, v3≥1, but 5∤N as fixed points. If 5|N, then it's not a fixed point. So we need to show that such numbers also lead to dead ends.

   Let's analyze the case where N is even, divisible by 3, and also divisible by 5. Then the three smallest divisors are 2,3,5 (since 4 does not divide because v2=1). So f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/5 = 31N/30. So N increases. Then M=f(N)=31·2^{a-2}·3^{b-1}·5·m? Actually N=2·3^b·5·m, with m coprime to 30. Then f(N)=31·3^{b-1}·5·m? Wait, N/2 = 3^b·5·m, N/3 = 2·3^{b-1}·5·m, N/5 = 2·3^b·m. Sum = 3^b·5·m + 2·3^{b-1}·5·m + 2·3^b·m = 3^{b-1}·m(5·3 + 2·5 + 2·3) = 3^{b-1}·m(15+10+6)=31·3^{b-1}·m. So f(N)=31·3^{b-1}·m. This is odd and not divisible by 3 if b=1? Actually if b=1, then f(N)=31·m, odd, not divisible by 3. If b≥2, then f(N) is divisible by 3. So then we have an odd multiple of 3 or an odd number not divisible by 3. Then as argued, odd numbers have f(N) < N, so the sequence will decrease. So eventually dead end.

   So numbers with v2=1, v3≥1, and 5|N increase to a number that is odd and not divisible by 3 (if b=1) or divisible by 3 (if b≥2). In either case, the next step will reduce the number. So overall, they cannot be infinite.

   Now, what about numbers with v2≥2, v3≥1, and 5|N? Then p=2, q=3, r=4 or 5? If v2≥2, then 4 divides N, so the order: 2,3,4. So f(N)=13N/12. Then M=13·2^{a-2}·3^{b-1}·5·m. Since a≥2, b≥1, and 5 divides, M is even? Actually v2(M)=a-2. If a=2, then v2(M)=0, so M is odd. If a≥3, then M is even. Also, v3(M)=b-1. If b=1, then v3(M)=0, so M is not divisible by 3. If b≥2, then M is divisible by 3. So we have various possibilities. If M is odd and not divisible by 3, then f(M)<M, so decreasing. If M is odd and divisible by 3, then f(M)<M, decreasing. If M is even and not divisible by 3, then f(M) < M (since even not divisible by 3 gives decreasing). If M is even and divisible by 3, then we are again in the even divisible by 3 case, and we can continue. This process will eventually reduce the exponent of 2 and 3 until we hit a number that is either odd or even not divisible by 3, and then it decreases. So eventually dead end.

   Thus, any a1 that is not in the good families leads to a dead end.

Proof outline:

1. Define f(N) as sum of three largest proper divisors of N (N has at least three proper divisors).
2. For N with at least three proper divisors, let the three smallest divisors >1 be p, q, r (p ≤ q ≤ r). Then the three largest proper divisors are N/p, N/q, N/r, so f(N)=N/p+N/q+N/r.

   - If v2(N)=1 and 5|N, then q=3, r=5, so f(N)=31N/30.
   - If v2(N)≥2 and 5|N, then q=3, r=4, so f(N)=13N/12 (still).
   - If N is odd and 3|N, then p=3, and since N is odd, the next divisors are at least 5, so f(N) ≤ N/3+N/5+N/7 < N.
   - If N is odd and 3∤N, then p≥5, and f(N) ≤ N/5+N/7+N/11 < N.
4. Hence, if a1 is not divisible by 3, then a2 < a1, and the sequence decreases until it reaches a number with fewer than three proper divisors. So infinite sequence impossible.
5. Therefore, a1 must be divisible by 3. Write a1=2^a·3^b·d, with d coprime to 6. Since a1 is divisible by 3, b≥1. Also, a1 is even? Not necessarily; a1 could be odd if a=0. But we already argued that if a=0, then a1 is odd and divisible by 3, so f(a1) < a1, and then the sequence decreases. So to avoid eventual dead end, we must have a≥1. Thus a1 is even. So a≥1.
6. Now, consider the case a is even. Then after a/2 steps, the number becomes odd (v2=0). That odd number, if divisible by 3, will have f < itself, and then the sequence decreases. Since the odd number cannot be a fixed point (fixed points are even), the sequence will eventually hit a prime or prime square. So infinite sequence impossible. Thus a must be odd.
7. So a is odd. Write a=2k+1 with k≥0. Then we apply the map T(N)=13N/12 repeatedly until v2 becomes 1. After k steps, we have N_k = 13^k·2·3^{b-k}·d. For this to be a fixed point (so that the sequence becomes constant), we need that N_k has the property that its three smallest divisors are 2,3,6. This requires that 5∤N_k, and also that b-k ≥ 1 (so that N_k is divisible by 3). Hence b ≥ k+1, i.e., v3(N) ≥ (v2(N)+1)/2. Also, we need that d is not divisible by 5 (since 5 could appear in d and cause N_k to be divisible by 5). Therefore, the necessary and sufficient conditions are: a1 is divisible by 6, 5∤a1, v2(a1) is odd, and v3(a1) ≥ (v2(a1)+1)/2.
8. Conversely, if a1 satisfies these conditions, then we can show that the sequence becomes constant after at most k steps, where k = (v2(a1)-1)/2. Thus the infinite sequence exists.

I'll write:

<solution>

Let $f(N)$ denote the sum of the three largest proper divisors of a positive integer $N$ that has at least three proper divisors. The sequence satisfies $a_{n+1}=f(a_n)$ and each $a_n$ has at least three proper divisors. We determine all $a_1$ for which such an infinite sequence exists.

First, we characterize $f(N)$ in terms of the smallest divisors. For $N$ with at least three proper divisors, let the divisors in increasing order be $1=d_1<d_2<\cdots<d_k=N$. Then the three largest proper divisors are $N/d_{k-1}=N/d_2$, $N/d_{k-2}=N/d_3$, $N/d_{k-3}=N/d_4$. Hence $f(N)=N/d_2+N/d_3+N/d_4$, where $d_2,d_3,d_4$ are the three smallest divisors larger than $1$.

Now, we analyze the behaviour of the iteration.

**Lemma 1.** If $N$ is not divisible by $3$, then $f(N)<N$.

*Proof.* If $N$ is even and not divisible by $3$, then $d_2=2$. Since $4$ does not necessarily divide $N$, the next divisor $d_3$ is at least $4$ if $4\mid N$, or at least $5$ otherwise. In either case, $d_4\ge 6$. Consequently,
\[
f(N)=\frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{d_3}+\frac{N}{d_4}\le \frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{4}+\frac{N}{5}= \frac{19}{20}N<N.
\]
If $N$ is odd and not divisible by $3$, then $d_2\ge 5$. Thus
\[
f(N)\le \frac{N}{5}+\frac{N}{7}+\frac{N}{11}<\frac{N}{5}+\frac{N}{5}+\frac{N}{5}= \frac{3}{5}N<N.
\]
Hence $f(N)<N$ in all cases.

**Lemma 2.** If $N$ is divisible by $3$ and $N$ is even, then $d_2=2$. Moreover,
- if $v_2(N)=1$ and $5\nmid N$, then $d_3=3$ and $d_4=6$;
- if $v_2(N)=1$ and $5\mid N$, then $d_3=3$ and $d_4=5$;
- if $v_2(N)\ge 2$, then $d_3=3$ and $d_4=4$.

*Proof.* Since $N$ is even, $2$ is a divisor. Because $3\mid N$, $3$ is also a divisor. If $v_2(N)=1$, then $4\nmid N$, so the next divisor after $2$ is $3$ (since $3$ is a divisor and no integer between $2$ and $3$). If $5\nmid N$, then the next divisor after $3$ is $6$ because $6\mid N$ (both $2$ and $3$ divide $N$) and no integer between $3$ and $6$ divides $N$ (the only candidate is $4$, which does not divide $N$ and $5$ is excluded). If $5\mid N$, then $5$ is a divisor and $5<6$, so $d_4=5$. If $v_2(N)\ge 2$, then $4\mid N$, and $4>3$, so the order is $2,3,4$. This completes the proof.

From Lemma 2 we obtain the explicit values:
\[
f(N)=
\begin{cases}
N, & \text{if } v_2(N)=1,\ 3\mid N,\ 5\nmid N;\\
\frac{13}{12}N, & \text{if } v_2(N)\ge 2,\ 3\mid N;\\
\frac{31}{30}N, & \text{if } v_2(N)=1,\ 3\mid N,\ 5\mid N.
\end{cases}
\]

Now, we consider an infinite sequence $\{a_n\}$.

If $a_1$ is not divisible by $3$, then by Lemma 1 $a_2<a_1$. Repeating, the sequence strictly decreases. Since all terms are positive integers, it must eventually reach a number with fewer than three proper divisors (a prime or a prime square), contradicting the hypothesis that each $a_n$ has at least three proper divisors. Therefore $a_1$ must be divisible by $3$.

Write $a_1=2^a\cdot3^b\cdot d$ with $d$ coprime to $6$; then $b\ge 1$ because $3\mid a_1$. If $a=0$, then $a_1$ is odd and divisible by $3$. In this case, as in Lemma 1, $f(a_1)<a_1$, so the sequence decreases and eventually reaches a dead end. Hence $a\ge 1$, i.e., $a_1$ is even.

Now, consider the exponent $a$. If $a$ is even, we repeatedly apply the map $N\mapsto \frac{13}{12}N$ as long as the number is even and $v_2\ge 2$. Because $a$ is even, after $a/2$ steps we obtain a number with $v_2=0$ (odd). That odd number is either divisible by $3$ or not; in either case, its image under $f$ is strictly smaller (by Lemma 1). Hence from that point on the sequence is strictly decreasing, and it cannot be infinite. Therefore $a$ must be odd.

Let $a=2k+1$ with $k\ge 0$. Then we can apply the transformation $N\mapsto f(N)=\frac{13}{12}N$ exactly $k$ times, provided the numbers remain even with $v_2\ge 2$ at each step. After $k$ steps we obtain
\[
N_k = 13^k\cdot 2\cdot 3^{b-k}\cdot d.
\]
For the sequence to be infinite, the process must eventually become constant. The only way to avoid a decreasing tail is that $N_k$ is a fixed point, i.e., $f(N_k)=N_k$. From Lemma 2, $N_k$ is a fixed point precisely when $v_2(N_k)=1$ and $3\mid N_k$ and $5\nmid N_k$. Since $v_2(N_k)=1$ and $3\mid N_k$ automatically if $b-k\ge 1$, the necessary and sufficient conditions are
\[
b-k\ge 1 \quad\text{and}\quad 5\nmid N_k.
\]
The second condition is equivalent to $5\nmid d$ because $13$ and $2$ and $3$ are not multiples of $5$. Hence $b\ge k+1$ and $5\nmid d$.

Conversely, if $a_1$ satisfies $a=2k+1$ (odd), $b\ge k+1$, and $5\nmid d$, then after $k$ steps we reach $N_k$ with $v_2=1$, $v_3\ge 1$, and $5\nmid N_k$, so $f(N_k)=N_k$ and the sequence becomes constant from that point onward; thus an infinite sequence exists.

Therefore, the possible values of $a_1$ are exactly the positive integers $N$ such that:
- $N$ is a multiple of $6$,
- $5$ does not divide $N$,
- the exponent of $2$ in $N$ is odd,
- the exponent of $3$ in $N$ is at least $(\text{exponent of }2+1)/2$.

Equivalently, $N$ can be written as
\[
N = 2^{2k+1}\cdot 3^{m}\cdot d,
\]
where $k\ge 0$, $m\ge k+1$, and $d$ is any positive integer coprime to $30$.

We now conclude that the set of all possible initial terms $a_1$ is
\[
\boxed{\{N\in\mathbb{N}: 6\mid N,\ 5\nmid N,\ v_2(N)\text{ is odd},\ v_3(N)\ge \frac{v_2(N)+1}{2}\}}.
\]

</solution>

Let $f(N)$ denote the sum of the three largest proper divisors of a positive integer $N$ that has at least three proper divisors. The sequence satisfies $a_{n+1}=f(a_n)$ and each $a_n$ has at least three proper divisors. We determine all $a_1$ for which such an infinite sequence exists.

First, we characterize $f(N)$ in terms of the smallest divisors. For $N$ with at least three proper divisors, let the divisors in increasing order be $1=d_1<d_2<\cdots<d_k=N$. Then the three largest proper divisors are $N/d_{k-1}=N/d_2$, $N/d_{k-2}=N/d_3$, $N/d_{k-3}=N/d_4$. Hence $f(N)=N/d_2+N/d_3+N/d_4$, where $d_2,d_3,d_4$ are the three smallest divisors larger than $1$.

Now, we analyze the behaviour of the iteration.

**Lemma 1.** If $N$ is not divisible by $3$, then $f(N)<N$.

*Proof.* If $N$ is even and not divisible by $3$, then $d_2=2$. Since $4$ does not necessarily divide $N$, the next divisor $d_3$ is at least $4$ if $4\mid N$, or at least $5$ otherwise. In either case, $d_4\ge 6$. Consequently,
\[
f(N)=\frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{d_3}+\frac{N}{d_4}\le \frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{4}+\frac{N}{5}= \frac{19}{20}N<N.
\]
If $N$ is odd and not divisible by $3$, then $d_2\ge 5$. Thus
\[
f(N)\le \frac{N}{5}+\frac{N}{7}+\frac{N}{11}<\frac{N}{5}+\frac{N}{5}+\frac{N}{5}= \frac{3}{5}N<N.
\]
Hence $f(N)<N$ in all cases.

**Lemma 2.** If $N$ is divisible by $3$ and $N$ is even, then $d_2=2$. Moreover,
- if $v_2(N)=1$ and $5\nmid N$, then $d_3=3$ and $d_4=6$;
- if $v_2(N)=1$ and $5\mid N$, then $d_3=3$ and $d_4=5$;
- if $v_2(N)\ge 2$, then $d_3=3$ and $d_4=4$.

*Proof.* Since $N$ is even, $2$ is a divisor. Because $3\mid N$, $3$ is also a divisor. If $v_2(N)=1$, then $4\nmid N$, so the next divisor after $2$ is $3$ (since $3$ is a divisor and no integer between $2$ and $3$). If $5\nmid N$, then the next divisor after $3$ is $6$ because $6\mid N$ (both $2$ and $3$ divide $N$) and no integer between $3$ and $6$ divides $N$ (the only candidate is $4$, which does not divide $N$ and $5$ is excluded). If $5\mid N$, then $5$ is a divisor and $5<6$, so $d_4=5$. If $v_2(N)\ge 2$, then $4\mid N$, and $4>3$, so the order is $2,3,4$. This completes the proof.

From Lemma 2 we obtain the explicit values:
\[
f(N)=
\begin{cases}
N, & \text{if } v_2(N)=1,\ 3\mid N,\ 5\nmid N;\\
\frac{13}{12}N, & \text{if } v_2(N)\ge 2,\ 3\mid N;\\
\frac{31}{30}N, & \text{if } v_2(N)=1,\ 3\mid N,\ 5\mid N.
\end{cases}
\]

Now, we consider an infinite sequence $\{a_n\}$.

If $a_1$ is not divisible by $3$, then by Lemma 1 $a_2<a_1$. Repeating, the sequence strictly decreases. Since all terms are positive integers, it must eventually reach a number with fewer than three proper divisors (a prime or a prime square), contradicting the hypothesis that each $a_n$ has at least three proper divisors. Therefore $a_1$ must be divisible by $3$.

Write $a_1=2^a\cdot3^b\cdot d$ with $d$ coprime to $6$; then $b\ge 1$ because $3\mid a_1$. If $a=0$, then $a_1$ is odd and divisible by $3$. In this case, as in Lemma 1, $f(a_1)<a_1$, so the sequence decreases and eventually reaches a dead end. Hence $a\ge 1$, i.e., $a_1$ is even.

Now, consider the exponent $a$. If $a$ is even, we repeatedly apply the map $N\mapsto \frac{13}{12}N$ as long as the number is even and $v_2\ge 2$. Because $a$ is even, after $a/2$ steps we obtain a number with $v_2=0$ (odd). That odd number is either divisible by $3$ or not; in either case, its image under $f$ is strictly smaller (by Lemma 1). Hence from that point on the sequence is strictly decreasing, and it cannot be infinite. Therefore $a$ must be odd.

Let $a=2k+1$ with $k\ge 0$. Then we can apply the transformation $N\mapsto f(N)=\frac{13}{12}N$ exactly $k$ times, provided the numbers remain even with $v_2\ge 2$ at each step. After $k$ steps we obtain
\[
N_k = 13^k\cdot 2\cdot 3^{b-k}\cdot d.
\]
For the sequence to be infinite, the process must eventually become constant. The only way to avoid a decreasing tail is that $N_k$ is a fixed point, i.e., $f(N_k)=N_k$. From Lemma 2, $N_k$ is a fixed point precisely when $v_2(N_k)=1$ and $3\mid N_k$ and $5\nmid N_k$. Since $v_2(N_k)=1$ and $3\mid N_k$ automatically if $b-k\ge 1$, the necessary and sufficient conditions are
\[
b-k\ge 1 \quad\text{and}\quad 5\nmid N_k.
\]
The second condition is equivalent to $5\nmid d$ because $13$ and $2$ and $3$ are not multiples of $5$. Hence $b\ge k+1$ and $5\nmid d$.

Conversely, if $a_1$ satisfies $a=2k+1$ (odd), $b\ge k+1$, and $5\nmid d$, then after $k$ steps we reach $N_k$ with $v_2=1$, $v_3\ge 1$, and $5\nmid N_k$, so $f(N_k)=N_k$ and the sequence becomes constant from that point onward; thus an infinite sequence exists.
